	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Early Islamic philosophy</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22">"Early Islamic philosophy"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Early+Islamic+philosophy%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<p><b>Early Islamic philosophy</b> or <b>classical Islamic philosophy</b> is a period of intense philosophical development beginning in the 2nd century AH of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a> (early 9th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>) and lasting until the 6th century AH (late 12th century CE). The period is known as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, and the achievements of this period had a crucial influence in the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>. For <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance Europe</a>, "Muslim maritime, agricultural, and technological innovations, as well as much East Asian technology via the Muslim world, made their way to western Europe in one of the largest technology transfers in world history.”<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This period starts with <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">al-Kindi</a> in the 9th century and ends with <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (Ibn Rushd) at the end of 12th century. The death of Averroes effectively marks the end of a particular discipline of Islamic philosophy usually called the <i><a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic Arabic School</a></i>, and philosophical activity declined significantly in Western <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic countries</a>, namely in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a> and North Africa, though it persisted for much longer in the Eastern countries, in particular <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_India" title="Islam in India">India</a> where several schools of philosophy continued to flourish: <a href="/wiki/Avicennism" title="Avicennism">Avicennism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illuminationist_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Illuminationist philosophy">Illuminationist philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufi_philosophy" title="Sufi philosophy">Mystical philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_theosophy" title="Transcendent theosophy">Transcendent theosophy</a>.
</p><p>Some of the significant achievements of early <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_philosophers" title="List of Muslim philosophers">Muslim philosophers</a> included the development of a strict <a href="/wiki/Scientific_citation" title="Scientific citation">science of citation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isnad" class="mw-redirect" title="Isnad">isnad</a> or "backing"; the development of a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">method of open inquiry</a> to disprove claims, the <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>, which could be generally applied to many types of questions (although which to apply it to is an ethical question); the willingness to both accept and challenge authority within the same process; recognition that science and philosophy are both subordinate to morality, and that moral choices should be made prior to any investigation or concern with either; the separation of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">kalam</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shariah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shariah">shariah</a>) during the early <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> period, a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> the distinction between religion and philosophy, marking the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular thought</a>; the beginning of a <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a> process; early ideas on <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>; the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, an important contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a>; the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal</a> <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_logic" class="mw-redirect" title="Inductive logic">inductive logic</a>; the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Social_philosophy" title="Social philosophy">social philosophy</a>, including the formulation of theories on <a href="/wiki/Structural_cohesion" title="Structural cohesion">social cohesion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_conflict" title="Social conflict">social conflict</a>; the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">philosophy of history</a>; the development of the <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_novel" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophical novel">philosophical novel</a> and the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a>;  and distinguishing between <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia Gaon</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_ben_Merwan_al-Mukkamas" class="mw-redirect" title="David ben Merwan al-Mukkamas">David ben Merwan al-Mukkamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, were influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">Mutazilite</a> work, particularly Avicennism and <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the use of <a href="/wiki/Empirical_method" class="mw-redirect" title="Empirical method">empirical methods</a> were inspired at least in part by Arabic translations of Greek, Jewish, Persian and Egyptian works <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">translated into Latin</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>, and taken during the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> in 1492.
</p><p>Early Islamic philosophy can be divided into clear sets of influences, branches, schools, and fields, as described below.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muhammad">Muhammad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Muhammad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The life of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sirah_Rasul_Allah" class="mw-redirect" title="Sirah Rasul Allah">sira</a> generated both the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a> (revelation) and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> (his daily utterances and discourses), during which philosophy was defined by Muslims as consisting in acceptance or rejection of his message. Together the sira and hadith constitute the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a> and are validated by <a href="/wiki/Isnad" class="mw-redirect" title="Isnad">isnad</a> ("backing") to determine the likely truth of the report of any given saying of Muhammad. Key figures are Imam <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja&#39;far al-Sadiq">Ja'far al-Sadiq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imam_Bukhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Imam Bukhari">Imam Bukhari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imam_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Imam Muslim">Imam Muslim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Tirmidhi" title="Al-Tirmidhi">Al-Tirmidhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Majah" title="Ibn Majah">Ibn Majah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dawud" title="Abu Dawud">Abu Dawud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Nasa%27i" title="Al-Nasa&#39;i">Al-Nasa'i</a>. This work, which was not completed until the 10th century, began shortly after <a href="/wiki/The_Farewell_Sermon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Farewell Sermon">The Farewell Sermon</a> in 631.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches">Branches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Branches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kalam">Kalam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kalam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a></div>
<p><b>Ilm al-Kalām</b>  (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">علم الكلام</span>&#8206;, literally the study of "speech" or "words") is the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophical</a> discipline of seeking <a href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic theology">theological</a> principles through <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a>. Kalām in Islamic practice relates to the discipline of seeking theological knowledge through debate and argument. A scholar of kalām is referred to as a <i>mutakallim</i> (plural <i>mutakallimiin</i>).
</p><p>With <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a>, questions about the <a href="/wiki/Sirah_Rasul_Allah" class="mw-redirect" title="Sirah Rasul Allah">sira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a>, as well as science (<a href="/wiki/Islamic_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic science">Islamic science</a>) and law (<a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a>), began to be investigated beyond the scope of Muhammad's beliefs. This period is characterized by emergence of <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a> and the first <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a>.  As the Sunnah became published and accepted, philosophy separate from <a href="/wiki/Muslim_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim theology">Muslim theology</a> was discouraged due to a lack of participants. During this period, traditions similar to <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">Socratic method</a> began to evolve, but philosophy remained subordinate to religion.
</p><p>Independent minds exploiting the methods of <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a> sought to investigate the doctrines of the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a>, which until then had been accepted in faith on the authority of divine revelation. One of the first debates was that between the <a href="/wiki/Qadarites" class="mw-redirect" title="Qadarites">Qadarites</a>, who affirmed <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Jabarites" class="mw-redirect" title="Jabarites">Jabarites</a></i>, who maintained the belief in <a href="/wiki/Fatalism" title="Fatalism">fatalism</a>.
</p><p>At the 2nd century of the <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">Hijra</a>, a new movement arose in the theological school of <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. A pupil, <a href="/wiki/Wasil_ibn_Ata" title="Wasil ibn Ata">Wasil ibn Ata</a> (AD 700–748), who was expelled from the school of <a href="/wiki/Hasan_of_Basra" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasan of Basra">Hasan of Basra</a> because his answers were contrary to then-orthodox Islamic tradition and became leader of a new school, and systematized the radical opinions of preceding sects, particularly those of the Qadarites. This new school was called <i><a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">Mutazilite</a></i> (from i'tazala, to separate oneself, to dissent) that lasted from the 8th to 10th centuries. Its principal dogmas were three:
</p>
<ol><li>God is an absolute unity, and no attribute can be ascribed to Him.</li>
<li>Man is a free agent. It is on account of these two principles that the Mu'tazilities designate themselves the "Partisans of Justice and Unity".</li>
<li>All knowledge necessary for the <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> of man emanates from his reason; humans could acquire knowledge before, as well as after, Revelation, by the sole light of reason. This fact makes knowledge obligatory upon all men, at all times, and in all places.</li></ol>
<p>The Mutazilities, compelled to defend their principles against the orthodox Islam of their day, looked for support in philosophy, and are one of the first to pursue a <a href="/wiki/Rational_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational theology">rational theology</a> called <i>Ilm-al-<a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic theology</a>); those professing it were called <i>Mutakallamin</i>. This appellation became the common name for all seeking philosophical demonstration in confirmation of religious principles. The first Mutakallamin had to debate both the orthodox and the non-<a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>, and they may be described as occupying the middle ground between those two parties. But subsequent generations were to large extent critical towards the Mutazilite school, especially after formation of the <a href="/wiki/Asharite" class="mw-redirect" title="Asharite">Asharite</a> concepts. Throughout history, the place of kalam in Islamic thought has been controversial. The vast majority of the early traditional Sunni Muslim scholars have either criticized or prohibited it. Jewish and Muslim Peripatetics, generally, stopped short in their respective Aristotelianism whenever there was danger of wounding orthodox religion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Falsafa">Falsafa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Falsafa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 9th century onward, owing to <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma&#39;mun">al-Ma'mun</a> and his successor, Greek philosophy was introduced among the <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arabs</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a> began to find able representatives among them; such were <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina (Avicenna)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Ibn Rushd (Averroës)</a>, all of whose fundamental principles were considered as criticized by the Mutakallamin. Another trend, represented by the <a href="/wiki/Brethren_of_Purity" title="Brethren of Purity">Brethren of Purity</a>, used Aristotelian language to expound a fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neopythagoreanism" title="Neopythagoreanism">Neopythagorean</a> world view.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_caliphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid caliphate">Abbasid caliphate</a> a number of thinkers and scientists, some of them <a href="/wiki/Heterodoxy" title="Heterodoxy">heterodox</a> Muslims or non-Muslims, played a role in transmitting Greek, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>, and other pre-Islamic knowledge to the <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>. They contributed to making <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> known in Christian <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Three speculative thinkers, <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina (Avicenna)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">al-Kindi</a>, combined <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> with other ideas introduced through Islam. They were considered by many as highly unorthodox and a few even described them as non-Islamic philosophers.
</p><p>From Spain Arabic philosophic literature was translated into <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, contributing to the development of modern European philosophy. The philosophers <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Moses Maimonides</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jew</a> born in <a href="/wiki/Al_Andalus" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Andalus">Muslim Spain</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> (born in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>), the father of <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>, were also important philosophers, though the latter did not identify himself as a <i>falsafa</i>, but rather a <i>kalam</i> author.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Some_differences_between_Kalam_and_Falsafa">Some differences between <i>Kalam</i> and <i>Falsafa</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Some differences between Kalam and Falsafa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aristotle attempted to demonstrate the unity of God; but from the view which he maintained, that matter was eternal, it followed that God could not be the <a href="/wiki/Creator_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Creator God">Creator</a> of the world. To assert that God's knowledge extends only to the general laws of the universe, and not to individual and accidental things, is tantamount to denying <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a>. One other part of Aristotle's theory shocked the faith of the Mutakallamin &#8212; the <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> theory of the soul. According to Aristotelianism, the human <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> is simply man's <a href="/wiki/Substantial_form" title="Substantial form">substantial form</a>, the set of properties that make matter into a living human body.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This seems to imply that the human soul cannot exist apart from the body. Indeed, Aristotle writes, "It is clear that the soul, or at least some parts of it (if it is divisible), cannot be separated from the body. [...] And thus, those have the right idea who think that the soul does not exist without the body."<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In Aristotelianism, at least one psychological force, the <a href="/wiki/Active_intellect" title="Active intellect">active intellect</a>, can exist apart from the body.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> However, according to many interpretations, the active intellect is a superhuman entity emanating from God and enlightening the human mind, not a part of any individual human soul.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Thus, Aristotle's theories seem to deny the immortality of the individual human soul.
</p><p>Wherefore the Mutakallamin had, before anything else, to establish a system of philosophy to demonstrate the creation of matter, and they adopted to that end the theory of atoms as enunciated by Democritus. They taught that atoms possess neither quantity nor extension. Originally atoms were created by God, and are created now as occasion seems to require. Bodies come into existence or die, through the aggregation or the sunderance of these atoms. But this theory did not remove the objections of philosophy to a creation of matter.
</p><p>For, indeed, if it be supposed that <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> commenced His work at a certain definite time by His "will", and for a certain definite object, it must be admitted that He was imperfect before accomplishing His will, or before attaining His object. In order to obviate this difficulty, the Motekallamin extended their theory of the atoms to <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">Time</a>, and claimed that just as <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">Space</a> is constituted of atoms and vacuum, Time, likewise, is constituted of small indivisible moments. The creation of the world once established, it was an easy matter for them to demonstrate the existence of a Creator, and that God is unique, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscient</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_protagonists_of_Falsafa_and_their_critics">Main protagonists of Falsafa and their critics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Main protagonists of Falsafa and their critics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 12th century saw the <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">apotheosis</a> of pure philosophy<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the decline of the Kalam, which later, being attacked by both the philosophers and the orthodox, perished for lack of champions. This supreme exaltation of philosophy may be attributed, in great measure, to Al-<a href="/wiki/Ghazali" title="Ghazali">Ghazali</a> (1005–1111) among the Persians, and to <a href="/wiki/Judah_ha-Levi" class="mw-redirect" title="Judah ha-Levi">Judah ha-Levi</a> (1140) among the Jews. It can be argued that the attacks directed against the philosophers by Al-Ghazali in his work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Incoherence_of_the_Philosophers" title="The Incoherence of the Philosophers">Tahafut al-Falasifa</a></i> (The Incoherence of the Philosophers), not only produced, by reaction, a current favorable to philosophy, but induced the philosophers themselves to profit by his criticism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They thereafter made their theories clearer and their logic closer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  The influence of this reaction brought forth the two greatest philosophers that the Islamic Peripatetic school ever produced,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> namely, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> (Avempace) and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Rushd" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Rushd">Ibn Rushd</a> (<a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>), both of whom undertook the defense of philosophy.
</p><p>Since no idea and no literary or philosophical movement ever germinated on Persian or Arabian soil without leaving its impress on the Jews, the Persian <a href="/wiki/Ghazali" title="Ghazali">Ghazali</a> found an imitator in the person of Judah ha-Levi. This poet also took upon himself to free his religion from what he saw as the shackles of speculative philosophy, and to this end wrote the "Kuzari", in which he sought to discredit all schools of philosophy alike. He passes severe censure upon the Mutakallamin for seeking to support religion by philosophy. He says, "I consider him to have attained the highest degree of perfection who is convinced of religious truths without having scrutinized them and reasoned over them" ("Kuzari", v.). Then he reduced the chief propositions of the Mutakallamin, to prove the unity of God, to ten in number, describing them at length, and concluding in these terms: "Does the <a href="/wiki/Kalam_(islamic_term)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalam (islamic term)">Kalam</a> give us more information concerning God and His attributes than the prophet did?" (Ib. iii. and iv.) Aristotelianism finds no favor in <a href="/wiki/Judah_ha-Levi" class="mw-redirect" title="Judah ha-Levi">Judah ha-Levi</a>'s eyes, for it is no less given to details and criticism; Neoplatonism alone suited him somewhat, owing to its appeal to his poetic temperament.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Ibn Rushd</a> (Averroës), the contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, closed the first great philosophical era of the Muslims. He was a defender of Aristotelian philosophy against <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash&#39;ari">Ash'ari</a> theologians led by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>. Averroes' philosophy was considered controversial in Muslim circles. The theories of Ibn Rushd do not differ fundamentally from those of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Tufail" title="Ibn Tufail">Ibn Tufail</a>, who only follow the teachings of Ibn Sina (Avicenna) and Al-Farabi. Like all Islamic Peripatetics, Ibn Rushd admits the hypothesis of the intelligence of the spheres and the hypothesis of universal emanation, through which motion is communicated from place to place to all parts of the universe as far as the supreme world&#8212;hypotheses which, in the mind of the Arabic philosophers, did away with the dualism involved in Aristotle's doctrine of pure energy and eternal matter. His ideas on the separation of philosophy and religion, further developed by the <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroist</a> school of philosophy, were later influential in the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Messeri_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messeri-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Najjar_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Najjar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>But while <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Sina">Ibn Sina</a> (Avicenna), and other Persian and Muslim philosophers hurried, so to speak, over subjects that trenched on religious dogmas, Ibn Rushd delighted in dwelling upon them with full particularity and stress. Thus he says, "Not only is matter eternal, but form is potentially inherent in matter; otherwise, it were a creation <i>ex <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nihilo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nihilo (page does not exist)">nihilo</a></i>" (Munk, "Mélanges", p.&#160;444). According to this theory, therefore, the existence of this world is not only a possibility, as Ibn Sina declared&#8212;in order to make concessions to the orthodox&#8212; but also a necessity.
</p><p>Driven from the Islamic schools, Islamic philosophy found a refuge with the Jews, to whom belongs the honor of having transmitted it to the Christian world. A series of eminent men&#8212;such as the <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Tibbon" title="Ibn Tibbon">Ibn Tibbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narboni" class="mw-redirect" title="Narboni">Narboni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gersonides" title="Gersonides">Gersonides</a>&#8212;joined in translating the Arabic philosophical works into Hebrew and commenting upon them. The works of Ibn Rushd especially became the subject of their study, due in great measure to Maimonides, who, in a letter addressed to his pupil <a href="/wiki/Joseph_ben_Judah" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph ben Judah">Joseph ben Judah</a>, spoke in the highest terms of Ibn Rushd's commentary.
</p><p>It should be mentioned that this depiction of intellectual tradition in Islamic Lands is mainly dependent upon what West could understand (or was willing to understand) from this long era. In contrast, there are some historians and philosophers who do not agree with this account and describe this era in a completely different way. Their main point of dispute is on the influence of different philosophers on Islamic Philosophy, especially the comparative importance of eastern intellectuals such as Ibn Sina and of western thinkers such as Ibn Rushd.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judeo-Islamic_philosophy">Judeo-Islamic philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Judeo-Islamic philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Islamic_philosophies_(800_-_1400)" class="mw-redirect" title="Judeo-Islamic philosophies (800 - 1400)">Judeo-Islamic philosophies (800 - 1400)</a></div>
<p>The oldest Jewish religio-philosophical work preserved is that of <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia Gaon</a> (892-942), <i><a href="/wiki/Emunoth_ve-Deoth" title="Emunoth ve-Deoth">Emunot ve-Deot</a></i>, "The Book of Beliefs and Opinions". In this work Saadia treats the questions that interested the Mutakallamin, such as the creation of matter, the unity of God, the divine attributes, the soul, etc. Saadia criticizes other philosophers severely. For Saadia there was no problem as to creation: God created the world <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_nihilo" title="Ex nihilo">ex nihilo</a></i>, just as the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> attests; and he contests the theory of the Mutakallamin in reference to atoms, which theory, he declares, is just as contrary to reason and religion as the theory of the philosophers professing the eternity of matter.
</p><p>To prove the unity of God, Saadia uses the demonstrations of the Mutakallamin. Only the attributes of essence (<i>sifat al-dhatia</i>) can be ascribed to God, but not the attributes of action (<i>sifat-al-fi'aliya</i>). The soul is a substance more delicate even than that of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">celestial spheres</a>. Here Saadia controverts the Mutakallamin, who considered the soul an "accident" '<i>arad</i> (compare <a href="/wiki/Guide_for_the_Perplexed" class="mw-redirect" title="Guide for the Perplexed">Guide for the Perplexed</a> i. 74), and employs the following one of their premises to justify his position: "Only a substance can be the substratum of an accident" (that is, of a non-essential property of things). Saadia argues: "If the soul be an accident only, it can itself have no such accidents as wisdom, joy, love", etc. Saadia was thus in every way a supporter of the Kalam; and if at times he deviated from its doctrines, it was owing to his religious views; just as the Jewish and Muslim Peripatetics stopped short in their respective Aristotelianism whenever there was danger of wounding orthodox religion.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools">Schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farabism">Farabism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Farabism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:200TengeNote.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/200TengeNote.jpg/220px-200TengeNote.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/200TengeNote.jpg/330px-200TengeNote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/200TengeNote.jpg/440px-200TengeNote.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:200TengeNote.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a> (Alfarabi), the founder of Farabism.</div></div></div>
<p>Al-Farabi (Alfarabi) was a founder of his own school of Islamic philosophy but which was later overshadowed by <a href="/wiki/Avicennism" title="Avicennism">Avicennism</a>. Al-Farabi's school of philosophy "breaks with the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> [... and ...] moves from <a href="/wiki/Islamic_metaphysics" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic metaphysics">metaphysics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">methodology</a>, a move that anticipates <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>", and "at the level of philosophy, Alfarabi unites theory and practice [... and] in the sphere of the <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> he liberates practice from theory". His <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonic</a> theology is also more than just metaphysics as rhetoric. In his attempt to think through the nature of a <a href="/wiki/Primum_movens" class="mw-redirect" title="Primum movens">First Cause</a>, Alfarabi discovers the limits of human knowledge".<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Farabi had great influence on science and philosophy for several centuries, and was widely considered second only to Aristotle in knowledge (alluded to by his title of "the Second Teacher") in his time. His work, aimed at synthesis of philosophy and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, paved the way for the work of <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina</a> (Avicenna).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Avicennism">Avicennism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Avicennism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Avicennism" title="Avicennism">Avicennism</a></div>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:18.0em;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding:0.4em 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_(980-1037)_CIPB2067.jpg" class="image" title="Portrait (1271)"><img alt="Portrait (1271)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/100px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="141" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/150px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/200px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1908" data-file-height="2692" /></a></td></tr><tr><td class="navbox-title" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em;padding:0.4em 0.25em 0;">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Avicenna" title="Category:Avicenna">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th class="navbox-title" style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;padding-top:0 0.25em 0.5em;font-size:175%;font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna<br /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"><span style="font-size:80%;">(Ibn Sīnā)</span></span></a></th></tr><tr><th class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding:0.1em">

</tr><tr><th class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding:0.1em">

</tr><tr><th class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding:0.1em">
Monuments</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.8em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Mausoleum" title="Avicenna Mausoleum">Avicenna Mausoleum</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Avicenne_Hospital" title="Avicenne Hospital">Avicenne Hospital</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Physician" title="The Physician">The Physician</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Physician_(2013_film)" title="The Physician (2013 film)">The Physician (2013 film)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avicenna_Cultural_and_Scientific_Foundation" title="Avicenna Cultural and Scientific Foundation">Avicenna Cultural and Scientific Foundation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scholars_Pavilion" title="Scholars Pavilion">Scholars Pavilion</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Avicenna_sidebar" title="Template:Avicenna sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Avicenna_sidebar" title="Template talk:Avicenna sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Avicenna_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s (Ibn Sina's) successful reconciliation between <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a>, Avicennism eventually became the leading school of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a> by the 12th century. Avicenna had become a central authority on philosophy by then, and several scholars in the 12th century commented on his strong influence at the time:<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>People nowadays [believe] that truth is whatever [Ibn Sina] says, that it is inconceivable for him to err, and that whoever contradicts him in anything he says cannot be rational.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Avicennism was also influential in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>, particularly his doctrines on the nature of the soul and his <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>-<a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> distinction, along with the debates and censure that they raised in <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic Europe</a>. This was particularly the case in Paris, where Avicennism was later <a href="/wiki/Proscribed" class="mw-redirect" title="Proscribed">proscribed</a> in 1210. Nevertheless, his <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and theory of knowledge influenced <a href="/wiki/William_of_Auvergne,_Bishop_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="William of Auvergne, Bishop of Paris">William of Auvergne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>, and his <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> influenced the thought of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AverroesColor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/AverroesColor.jpg/220px-AverroesColor.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/AverroesColor.jpg/330px-AverroesColor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/AverroesColor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="352" data-file-height="276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AverroesColor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (Ibn Rushd), the founder of <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Averroism">Averroism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Averroism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a></div>
<p>Averroes (Ibn Rushd) is most famous for his commentaries on <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s works and for writing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Incoherence_of_the_Incoherence" title="The Incoherence of the Incoherence">The Incoherence of the Incoherence</a></i> in which he defended the <i>falasifa</i> against <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a>'s <i>The Incoherence of the Philosophers</i>. While he had very little influence in the Islamic world, which was then dominated by Avicennian philosophy and <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash&#39;ari">Ash'ari</a> theology, Averroism became very influential in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>, especially among the <a href="/wiki/Scholastics" class="mw-redirect" title="Scholastics">Scholastics</a>. Averroism eventually led to the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Messeri_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messeri-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Najjar_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Najjar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> for which Ibn Rushd is considered as the founding father of secular thought in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Fakhry_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fakhry-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of "<a href="/wiki/Existence_precedes_essence" title="Existence precedes essence">existence precedes essence</a>", a key foundational concept of <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, can also be found in the works of Averroes, as a reaction to <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s concept of "<a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> precedes <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics" title="Islamic ethics">Islamic ethics</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_philosophy">Environmental philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Environmental philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Perhaps due to resource scarcity in most Islamic nations, there was an emphasis on limited (and some claim also sustainable) use of <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">natural capital</a>, i.e. producing land. Traditions of <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">haram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hima_(environmental_protection)" title="Hima (environmental protection)">hima</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> were expressions of strong social obligations to stay within <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> and to preserve the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural environment</a> as an obligation of khalifa or "stewardship".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad is considered a pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> for his teachings on <a href="/wiki/Environmental_preservation" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental preservation">environmental preservation</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadiths</a> on <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_philosophy" title="Environmental philosophy">environmental philosophy</a> were compiled in the "Book of Agriculture" of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_Bukhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahih Bukhari">Sahih Bukhari</a></i>, which included the following saying:<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"There is none amongst the believers who plants a tree, or sows a seed, and then a bird, or a person, or an animal eats thereof, but it is regarded as having given a charitable gift [for which there is great recompense]."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Several such statements concerning the environment are also found in the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a>, such as the following:<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"And there is no animal in the earth nor bird that flies with its two wings, but that they are communities like yourselves."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The earliest known treatises dealing with <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic medicine">Arabic medical treatises</a> written by <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">al-Kindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">Qusta ibn Luqa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Razi" title="Al-Razi">al-Razi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Al-Jazzar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Al-Jazzar">Ibn Al-Jazzar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Tamimi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Tamimi">al-Tamimi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Masihi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Masihi">al-Masihi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Ridwan" title="Ali ibn Ridwan">Ali ibn Ridwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Jumay%E2%80%98" title="Ibn Jumay‘">Ibn Jumay‘</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Israeli_ben_Solomon" title="Isaac Israeli ben Solomon">Isaac Israeli ben Solomon</a>, Ibn al-Quff, and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a>. Their works covered a number of subjects related to pollution such as <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a> mishandling, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_assessment" title="Environmental impact assessment">environmental impact assessments</a> of certain localities.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Cordoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a> also had the first <a href="/wiki/Waste_container" title="Waste container">waste containers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste disposal">waste disposal</a> facilities for <a href="/wiki/Litter" title="Litter">litter</a> collection.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_ethics">Medical ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medical ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">Bimaristan</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ethical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethical">ethical</a> standards of Muslim physicians was first laid down in the 9th century by Ishaq bin Ali Rahawi, who wrote the <i>Adab al-Tabib</i> (<i>Conduct of a Physician</i>), the first treatist dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">medical ethics</a>. He regarded physicians as "guardians of souls and bodies", and wrote twenty chapters on various topics related to medical ethics, including:<sup id="cite_ref-Ethics_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethics-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>What the <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> must avoid and beware of</li>
<li>The manners of <a href="/wiki/Visitor" title="Visitor">visitors</a></li>
<li>The care of <a href="/wiki/Cure" title="Cure">remedies</a> by the physician</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Dignity" title="Dignity">dignity</a> of the medical <a href="/wiki/Profession" title="Profession">profession</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Test_(assessment)" title="Test (assessment)">examination</a> of physicians</li>
<li>The removal of corruption among physicians</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanism">Humanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Humanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many medieval Muslim thinkers pursued <a href="/wiki/Humanistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Humanistic">humanistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rational</a> and scientific <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourses</a> in their search for knowledge, meaning and <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" class="mw-redirect" title="Value (personal and cultural)">values</a>. A wide range of Islamic writings on love <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>, history and <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_theology" title="Philosophical theology">philosophical theology</a> show that medieval Islamic thought was open to the humanistic ideas of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a>, occasional <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodman_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another reason the Islamic world flourished during the Middle Ages was an early emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, as summarized by al-Hashimi (a cousin of Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma&#39;mun">al-Ma'mun</a>) in the following letter to one of the religious opponents he was attempting to <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">convert</a> through <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Bring forward all the arguments you wish and say whatever you please and speak your mind freely. Now that you are safe and free to say whatever you please appoint some arbitrator who will impartially judge between us and lean only towards the truth and be free from the empery of passion, and that arbitrator shall be <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">Reason</a>, whereby God makes us responsible for our own rewards and punishments. Herein I have dealt justly with you and have given you full security and am ready to accept whatever decision Reason may give for me or against me. For "There is no compulsion in religion" (<a href="/wiki/Al-Baqara" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Baqara">Qur'an 2</a>:256) and I have only invited you to accept our faith willingly and of your own accord and have pointed out the hideousness of your present belief. Peace be with you and the blessings of God!
</p></blockquote>
<p>Certain aspects of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a> has its roots in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">medieval Islamic world</a>, including the "art of <i><a href="/wiki/Dictation_(exercise)" title="Dictation (exercise)">dictation</a></i>, called in Latin, <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_dictaminis" title="Ars dictaminis">ars dictaminis</a></i>,"
and "the humanist attitude toward <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">Logic in Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<p>In early Islamic philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> played an important role. <a href="/wiki/Islamic_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic law">Islamic law</a> placed importance on formulating standards of argument, which gave rise to a novel approach to logic in <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a>, but this approach was later displaced by ideas from <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a> with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mu%27tazili" class="mw-redirect" title="Mu&#39;tazili">Mu'tazili</a> philosophers, who highly valued <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></i>. The works of Hellenistic-influenced Islamic philosophers were crucial in the reception of Aristotelian logic in medieval Europe, along with the commentaries on the <i>Organon</i> by <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>. The works of <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a> and other Muslim logicians who often criticized and corrected Aristotelian logic and introduced their own forms of logic, also played a central role in the subsequent development of European logic during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Routledge_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For the Islamic philosophers, logic included not only the study of formal patterns of <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a> and their validity but also elements of the philosophy of language and even of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>. Because of territorial disputes with the Arabic grammarians, Islamic philosophers were very interested in working out the relationship between logic and language, and they devoted much discussion to the question of the subject matter and aims of logic in relation to reasoning and speech. In the area of formal logical analysis, they elaborated upon the theory of <a href="/wiki/Terminology" title="Terminology">terms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proposition_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Proposition (philosophy)">propositions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogisms</a> as formulated in Aristotle's Categories, De interpretatione and Prior Analytics. In the spirit of Aristotle, they believed that all rational argument can be  reduced to a syllogism, and they regarded syllogistic theory as the focal point of logic. Even poetics was considered as a syllogistic art in some fashion by most of the major Islamic Aristotelians.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Important developments made by Muslim logicians included the development of "Avicennian logic" as a replacement of Aristotelian logic. <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s <a href="/wiki/System_of_logic" class="mw-redirect" title="System of logic">system of logic</a> was responsible for the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_syllogism" title="Hypothetical syllogism">hypothetical syllogism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal</a> <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive logic</a>. Other important developments in early Islamic philosophy include the development of a strict <a href="/wiki/Scientific_citation" title="Scientific citation">science of citation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isnad" class="mw-redirect" title="Isnad">isnad</a> or "backing", and the development of a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method of open inquiry</a> to disprove claims, the <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>, which could be generally applied to many types of questions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic_in_Islamic_law_and_theology">Logic in Islamic law and theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Logic in Islamic law and theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early forms of <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogical reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a> and categorical <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a> were introduced in <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a> (Islamic jurisprudence), <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> (Islamic law) and <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a> (Islamic theology) from the 7th century with the process of <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">Qiyas</a></i>, before the Arabic translations of Aristotle's works. Later during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, there was a logical debate among Islamic philosophers, logicians and theologians over whether the term <i>Qiyas</i> refers to analogical reasoning, inductive reasoning or categorical syllogism. Some Islamic scholars argued that <i>Qiyas</i> refers to inductive reasoning, which <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> (994-1064) disagreed with, arguing that <i>Qiyas</i> does not refer to inductive reasoning, but refers to categorical syllogism in a <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">real</a> sense and analogical reasoning in a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphorical</a> sense. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a> (1058–1111) (and in modern times, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Muhammad_Asem_al-Maqdisi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Muhammad Asem al-Maqdisi">Abu Muhammad Asem al-Maqdisi</a>) argued that <i>Qiyas</i> refers to analogical reasoning in a real sense and categorical syllogism in a metaphorical sense. Other Islamic scholars at the time, however, argued that the term <i>Qiyas</i> refers to both analogical reasoning and categorical syllogism in a real sense.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotelian_logic">Aristotelian logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aristotelian logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first original Arabic writings on logic were produced by <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">al-Kindi</a> (Alkindus) (805–873), who produced a summary on earlier logic up to his time. The first writings on logic with non-Aristotelian elements was produced by <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Farabi</a> (Alfarabi) (873–950), who discussed the topics of future <a href="/wiki/Contingent" class="mw-redirect" title="Contingent">contingents</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">number</a> and relation of the <a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)">categories</a>, the relation between <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a>, and non-Aristotelian forms of <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He is also credited for categorizing logic into two separate groups, the first being "idea" and the second being "<a href="/wiki/Logical_argument" class="mw-redirect" title="Logical argument">proof</a>".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (1126–98) was the last major logician from <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>, who wrote the most elaborate commentaries on Aristotelian logic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Avicennian_logic">Avicennian logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Avicennian logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> (980-1037) developed his own system of logic known as "Avicennian logic" as an alternative to Aristotelian logic. By the 12th century, Avicennian logic had replaced Aristotelian logic as the dominant system of logic in the Islamic world.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first criticisms of Aristotelian logic were written by <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> (980–1037), who produced independent treatises on logic rather than commentaries. He criticized the logical school of Baghdad for their devotion to Aristotle at the time. He investigated the theory of <a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classification_theorems" class="mw-redirect" title="Classification theorems">classification</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quantification_(logic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantification (logic)">quantification</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(mathematical_logic)" title="Predicate (mathematical logic)">predicates</a> of categorical <a href="/wiki/Proposition_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Proposition (philosophy)">propositions</a>, and developed an original theory on "<a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal</a> <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal</a>" syllogism. Its premises included <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_modifier" title="Grammatical modifier">modifiers</a> such as "at all times", "at most times", and "at some time".
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> (980-1037) often relied on <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a> in philosophy, he used a different approach in medicine. Ibn Sina contributed inventively to the development of <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive logic</a>, which he used to pioneer the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Syndrome" title="Syndrome">syndrome</a>. In his medical writings, Avicenna was the first to describe the <a href="/wiki/Mill%27s_Methods" title="Mill&#39;s Methods">methods of agreement, difference and concomitant variation</a> which are critical to inductive logic and the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodman_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> (994-1064) wrote the <i>Scope of Logic</i>, in which he stressed on the importance of <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sense</a> <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> as a source of knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Herald_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herald-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> (Algazel) (1058–1111) had an important influence on the use of logic in theology, making use of Avicennian logic in <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Despite the logical sophistication of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a>, the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash&#39;ari">Ash'ari</a> school in the 12th century slowly suffocated original work on logic in much of the Islamic world, though logic continued to be studied in some Islamic regions such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">Fakhr al-Din al-Razi</a> (b. 1149) criticised Aristotle's "<a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">first figure</a>" and developed a form of <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive logic</a>, foreshadowing the system of inductive logic developed by <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> (1806–1873). Systematic refutations of Greek logic were written by the <a href="/wiki/Illuminationist_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Illuminationist philosophy">Illuminationist school</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Shahab_al-Din_Suhrawardi" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahab al-Din Suhrawardi">Shahab al-Din Suhrawardi</a> (1155–1191), who developed the idea of "decisive necessity", an important innovation in the history of logical philosophical speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-Herald_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herald-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Another systematic refutation of Greek logic was written by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> (1263&#8211;1328), the <i>Ar-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin</i> (<i>Refutation of Greek Logicians</i>), where he argued against the usefulness, though not the validity, of the <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and in favour of <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Iqbal_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_metaphysics" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic metaphysics">Islamic metaphysics</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmological_and_ontological_arguments">Cosmological and ontological arguments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cosmological and ontological arguments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_the_Truthful" title="Proof of the Truthful">Proof of the Truthful</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s proof for the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Proof_of_the_Truthful" title="Proof of the Truthful">Proof of the Truthful</a>", was the first <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological argument</a>, which he proposed in the <i>Metaphysics</i> section of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morewedge_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morewedge-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> This was the first attempt at using the method of <a href="/wiki/A_priori_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="A priori (philosophy)">a priori proof</a>, which utilizes intuition and reason alone. Avicenna's proof of God's existence is unique in that it can be classified as both a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">cosmological argument</a> and an ontological argument. "It is ontological insofar as ‘necessary existence’ in intellect is the first basis for arguing for a Necessary Existent". The proof is also "cosmological insofar as most of it is taken up with arguing that contingent existents cannot stand alone and must end up in a Necessary Existent."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinction_between_essence_and_existence">Distinction between essence and existence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Islamic_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Distinction between essence and existence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Islamic philosophy, imbued as it is with <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Islamic theology</a>, distinguishes more clearly than <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> the difference between <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>. Whereas existence is the domain of the <a href="/wiki/Contingent" class="mw-redirect" title="Contingent">contingent</a> and the accidental, essence endures within a <a href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a> beyond the accidental. This was first described by <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s works on <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, who was himself influenced by <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Farabi</a>.
</p><p>Some <i>orientalists</i> (or those particularly influenced by Thomist scholarship) argued that Avicenna was the first to view existence (<i>wujud</i>) as an accident that happens to the essence (<i>mahiyya</i>).  However, this aspect of ontology is not the most central to the distinction that Avicenna established between essence and existence.  One cannot therefore make the claim that Avicenna was the proponent of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Essentialism" title="Essentialism">essentialism</a> <i>per se</i>, given that existence (<i>al-wujud</i>) when thought of in terms of necessity would ontologically translate into a notion of the <i>Necessary-Existent-due-to-Itself</i> (<i>wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi</i>), which is without description or definition, and particularly without quiddity or essence (<i>la mahiyya lahu</i>).
